Nura stepped down carefully. The plank was wet and slimed with old algae. Shamima caught her elbow before she slipped and gave her a look that said she was already regretting this.

The channel between chars was not wide, but it had the authority of something that could still decide to take a person. The engine started on the third pull, coughing diesel over the river's stale-sweet smell. Water hyacinth drifted by in broken mats. Dawn widened, and Kadam Char pulled back into low shapes of tin and bamboo, the school wall a pale smudge, the mosque dome barely catching light. Nura kept her eyes on it until the bend took it away. Only then did she unfold the paper in her hand and look once more at the name she had copied the night before from her birth record. The name on it was Monir, just that, with a father's line that might have been as false as the rest.

Between what Halima had let slip in the bus-stand lodge and what Sufia had muttered later while pouring tea as if she did not care whether Nura heard or not, Nura had gotten three usable things: the name, the neighboring char, and dredger work. That was more than Halima had intended to give. It was enough for a morning's rebellion and maybe enough to kill a fantasy cleanly, which was more mercy than Nura had been offered so far.

Shamima watched the folded slip in Nura's fingers. "You still haven't told me what you'll do if you find him."

"Ask him some questions."

"You're crossing a river for some questions. You could ask questions at home and get shouted at free."

Nura looked at the current where the engine chewed it white. "At home they answer around the thing. I want the thing."

Shamima snorted, then tucked the sewing-machine belt deeper into her bag. "Men are never the thing. They're only the hole after."

It was the kind of sentence Shamima said without posing it as wisdom. She said it while checking whether the newspaper around her belt had gotten wet, the same way she might have said the water would rise after noon. Nura almost smiled. Then she didn't. She touched the small burn mark on her wrist with her thumb and looked ahead.

The neighboring char woke noisier than Kadam Char. By the time their boat nudged into the mud, two boys were dragging a basket of duck feed up the bank, a woman was rinsing blackened tea glasses in a bucket gone gray with old sugar, and someone farther in was beating a rug against a bamboo rail with the rhythm of an argument. The ghat smelled of river slime, green chili frying in old oil, and the ammoniac edge of goat droppings warming under the first sun. A row of tea stalls had been made from tin, tarpaulin, and stubbornness. Men were already standing in lungis with their cups tilted from the saucers, talking about sand rates and fuel as if they had been at it all night.

Shamima jerked her chin toward the second stall. "Ask Momena. She knows who owes what before they know it themselves."

Momena was thick-wristed and quick, maybe forty, maybe older, with a faded orange shawl thrown over one shoulder and the top joint of her left ring finger missing as neatly as if a machine had bitten it off and then lost interest. She kept a carpenter's pencil tucked into the knot of her hair and wrote credit marks on the inside flap of a biscuit carton nailed beside the kettle. When Nura stepped up, Momena did not ask what she wanted. She looked once at Nura's face, once at the careful way she held her folded paper, and pushed a bench with her knee.

"Sit first. If you fall in this heat, I won't carry you."

Nura sat because refusal would have made her look younger.

"I'm looking for someone," she said.

"Everybody is. Tea?"

"No."

"Then you'll need a name that earns the trouble."

Nura unfolded the slip. "Monir. He worked here some years back. With dredger people, I heard."

Momena's hand stopped over the kettle handle, not with surprise but with the quick inventory of a woman deciding which Monir, which debt, which ruin.

"Tall?" she asked. "Not truly tall. Just thin enough to look taller. Hair always wanting oil and never getting it?"

Nura had no answer, and Momena saw that at once.

"You're not from his bed," Momena said.

The bench felt suddenly too narrow. "No."

"Then what are you from?"

Nura forced herself not to lower her voice. "He's on my birth paper."

Momena looked at her a long moment. Her eyes were not soft. They were not cruel either. They were working.

"Paper," she said at last. "All right." She lifted the kettle, poured tea through a cloth strainer into two glasses anyway, and set one in front of Nura. "Drink. You look like you'll faint from pride before hunger gets you. If it's that Monir, go to Noorjahan's place behind the dry pond. Third house with the blue drum outside. He slept there on and off one flood season and left owing me thirteen days of tea, four cigarettes he said he'd pay after wages, and one enamel cup he swore he'd only borrowed. If you want the part of him that stayed put, women kept it. Men only know which boat took him last."

Shamima took the second glass before Momena changed her mind. "Still writing everybody down?" she asked.

"What else would I do? Men think forgetting is a profession."

She said it while marking something on the biscuit carton with the carpenter's pencil. A little boy came up to the stall carrying a hen upside down. Momena wiped his nose with the end of her shawl without breaking her stare from Nura.

"Go before Noorjahan leaves for the fish yard," she said. "And if she's skinning koi already, don't stand in the doorway talking like a police clerk. Sit and ask clean."

The path to Noorjahan's house cut past a pond that had dried to cracked green slime and mosquito skin, then through a strip of low huts where fish were laid on mats to salt. Dogs slept in the shade under bamboo racks. Women stepped around one another with basins on their hips, speaking in that fast half-shared shorthand Nura always heard among people who had too much to do and no time to turn themselves into speeches. One of them pointed with her chin when Shamima asked for Noorjahan, then called after them, "Tell her I kept the chilies inside. Rain's coming later." Another answered from a doorway that the sky said otherwise. They argued without stopping their hands.

Noorjahan's house had a blue plastic water drum outside and a line of school shirts drying from the eaves, all of them patched at the collar. She was sitting on a low wooden stool in the yard with a basin between her knees, scraping silver skin off tiny fish with the back of a spoon. The spoon must once have belonged to a better set. Its handle had flowers worn down to almost nothing. Noorjahan herself was not old, perhaps not even thirty, but hard use had taken the soft guesses off her face. Her hair was braided and coiled tight. One heel pressed the stool rung; the other foot kept time against the earth as she worked. A little girl beside her was trying to braid red thread into a doll made from rolled cloth and muttering instructions to it under her breath.

When Nura said Monir's name, Noorjahan did not drop the spoon or freeze or perform the shock of memory. She scraped one more fish clean, flicked the scales from her fingers, and only then looked up.

"Which Monir?" she asked.

Shamima, who had clearly decided she would rather finish this once than ferry Nura back half-satisfied, answered before Nura could trip over herself. "The dredger one. Thin. Used to drink tea at Momena's and sleep here sometimes."

"Sleep everywhere sometimes," Noorjahan said.

The little girl looked up. "Ma, is this about the man who stole the mug?"

"Go inside, Juthi."

"I'm braiding."

"Take the doll and braid inside."

Juthi scooped up the limp cloth doll and went, but slowly enough to hear more if she could. Noorjahan wiped her fish-slick hands on the edge of her sari and nodded to the overturned bucket by the wall. "Sit. If this is going to be about papers, I don't want you fainting and making extra work."

Nura sat. The bucket flexed under her weight. Heat from the yard had already climbed into the plastic. She could smell fish blood, dish soap, and the cumin someone nearby had just thrown into hot oil.

"I'm from Kadam Char," she said. "His name is on my birth record. I needed to know if he's alive. If he has any papers. If..." She stopped. The sentence had run out into something she did not want to say in front of a stranger.

Noorjahan rescued her from finishing it, not kindly but efficiently. "If he can stand up and call himself your father for the government. That kind of thing."

Nura hated how quickly her face warmed. "Something like that."

Noorjahan leaned back and let out a short breath through her nose. "Then you've spent your boat fare badly."

There was no meanness in it, only the flatness of a verdict long familiar to its speaker.

"He stayed here one wet season," Noorjahan said. "Not every night. He worked near the dredger cut when there was work and vanished when there wasn't. He said he had people upriver. He said he didn't. He said he'd bring sugar after payday. He said a lot. When fever took him, I boiled him coriander water and Momena sent tea on credit. When his wages came late, he borrowed from me, from Momena, from Ruksana next door for kerosene. He left a lungi and one rusted shaving blade and took the money. That's the shape of him I knew."

Ruksana herself appeared in the doorway next door as if summoned by the sound of her name. She was broad across the hips, with a sunflower-printed housecoat under her sari and henna still clinging in orange ghosts to two fingernails. She had a baby balanced on one side, not her own by the look of how carelessly familiar the child's weight sat on her.

"He borrowed from me for kerosene and never returned the bottle," she said. "Say that properly if you're listing him."

Noorjahan clicked her tongue. "See? Women remember the real estate of a man. Bottle, blade, cup, debt."

Ruksana shifted the baby higher and studied Nura. "Who's she to him?"

Nura made herself say it cleanly. "He's on my papers."

Ruksana's face changed, but only around the mouth. The change was not pity or surprise. It was something like recognition of a kind she wished there were less of.

"Come in out of the yard, then," she said. "If you're going to hear ugly things, hear them with water in your hand."

Inside Noorjahan's room it was darker and cooler. The packed floor smelled faintly of damp rice sack. A shelf held a steel plate, two schoolbooks with warped corners, a jar of cumin, and a cracked bottle of cough syrup with no cap. Juthi was on the bed platform, still braiding the doll's red threads. Ruksana set the baby down beside her and poured water from a jug into an aluminum tumbler. Nobody asked Nura if she wanted it. They handed it to her the way women handed one another necessary things.

Noorjahan did not sit this time. She stood by the doorway, one hand on the frame.

"You're not the first girl to come asking after a man's name," she said. "You're only the first one this week."

Shamima barked a laugh before she could stop herself. "Only this week."

"I don't mean him," Noorjahan said. "I mean men. They spread trouble wide. We do the walking."

She nodded toward the house across the lane, where an elderly woman was shaking water from a sari with both arms. "When Monir got fever, that one watched my girl while I went for tablets. When he went missing after taking money, Ruksana told Momena before Momena sent her nephew to ask the dredger boys. When my roof tore in the storm after, Jharna loaned me plastic until my brother came. Nobody asked whose wife I was before helping. They asked if the water was already on my bedding. That's the only useful question most days."

Nura drank the water. It was warm and metallic. She did not know why that simple line undid something in her more than the debt list had. Perhaps because it made Halima smaller without making her lesser. Halima had always seemed to carry the whole logic of women's survival alone inside her body, as if every compromise began and ended in that house. Here the work was spread through cups of tea, watched children, borrowed kerosene, a neighbor knowing which roof leaked first. It was not noble or pretty. It was simply built.

"Did he have papers?" Nura asked.

Noorjahan's mouth bent. "He had a voter slip once. He kept it in his shirt pocket until the shirt mildewed. After that I don't know. When labor men came counting hands for the dredger contractor, he gave his name fast and his father's name faster. Could have been true. Could have been Tuesday."

Ruksana said, "Ask Babul at the pump yard. He was on that cut then. If anyone saw which boat took Monir, Babul did. He sees everything and keeps it because nobody else wants it."

Juthi, from the bed platform, said, "He whistles with his nose when he sleeps."

Noorjahan pointed at her daughter without turning. "That is not useful."

"It is if she's going to find him."

"She's not going to find him."

The words landed in the room with a rough certainty that did not feel theatrical because nobody there had arranged themselves around Nura's feelings before speaking. The child returned to braiding. Ruksana lifted the baby and bounced him once on her hip until he hiccupped quiet. Shamima adjusted her scarf and did not look at Nura.

Nura put the tumbler down carefully. "Did he say where he came from? Anything true?"

Noorjahan scratched at a fish scale stuck to her wrist. "He said once that the river had taken his land. He said once that his brothers had. He said once there was a wife who wouldn't take him back. I never met the land, the brothers, or the wife. What I met was a man who could make a face like a hungry boy and borrow the price of tea with it."

"And you waited for him?" Nura asked before she could stop herself.

It was a rude question. She knew it as soon as it left.

But Noorjahan only shrugged. "For a while. Not because he was worth waiting for. Because when a man says he's coming back with wages and you already spent your own on his fever tablets, you wait long enough to find out whether you're stupid or robbed. Turns out you can be both and still need to cook rice."

Ruksana laughed, open and unashamed. Even Noorjahan smiled for half a second.

"Go to Babul," Noorjahan said. "Then eat something before you fall over. If you go home empty, at least go home standing."

The pump yard lay near the cut where the channel widened and the bank had been hardened with sandbags that were already splitting at the seams. Two engines sat open under a shade roof, each ribbed with hoses, bolts, and rags black with grease. Men moved around them in the detached hurry of workers on someone else's schedule. The dredger itself was farther out, a rusted hulk moored in brown water, its pipe arcing to the bank like a stiff neck.

Babul was easy to pick out. He was short and square, with one ear folded in on itself from some old infection and a pair of cracked eyeglasses he kept pushing back up with the inside of his wrist because both hands were slick with oil. He listened to Shamima's question while tightening a nut with a wrench so worn it no longer had any paint on it.

"Monir," he said, and spat to the side. "Which Monir?"

Shamima gave up first. "The useless one."

Babul barked out a laugh. A younger worker behind him, all elbows and beard fluff, said, "That narrows nothing."

Nura stepped forward. "Thin. Used to work this cut years ago. Stayed on the char behind the dry pond. Maybe went downriver with a dredger crew."

Babul's laugh faded. He squinted at her over the top of his glasses. "You're kin?"

"He's on my papers," Nura said again, and hated the phrase more each time.

Babul wiped his hands on a rag already too dirty to help. "Ah."

That one sound held more understanding than sympathy. He jerked his chin toward an upturned drum. "Sit if you want. Or don't. He worked here some, yes. Not steady. Nobody steady stayed on that cut. Contractor paid advances to men who looked like they had no one to chase after them, then changed crews as soon as the bank moved. Monir took an advance and got on a southbound dredger one season when the sand shifted. Might have gone as far as Sirajganj side. Might have jumped off at the next market. Might have been thrown off for drinking. There was talk of an accident near a propeller, but there is always talk. River work eats men and rumor spits out bones."

The younger worker, whose name turned out to be Selim when somebody shouted for him, leaned against the engine frame. He had rolled his lungi up to one thigh and was using a blade to scrape mud from under his toenails with complete concentration.

"He had a blue bag," Selim said. "Small one. That's all I remember."

"Everybody had a blue bag," Babul said.

"Then he had one too."

"Did he send money back anywhere?" Nura asked.

Babul gave her the kind of look older men gave when they thought a question had been asked from too far inside paper. "Back where?"

She had no answer. The word itself shamed her. Back required an origin. A place that claimed a person even while he was gone. Everything she had come for was tied up in that missing word.

Selim scratched one last line free of mud and flicked it away. "Men like that sent money only if a woman stood at the ghat the same day wages landed. Even then half of it turned into tea and cards before the boat left."

Babul said, "If you're needing him for some government thing, don't. Even alive, he'd be no use. Those floating crews change names the way they change landing points. Papers get wet. Cards go missing. Half the fathers in those registers could be one man wearing three stories."

The sentence should have angered her more. Instead it settled into her like the hard edge of an object finally found under muddy water. Even alive, he'd be no use.

That was what she had crossed for. It was not romance or reunion. It was use.

She felt suddenly embarrassed by the careful fold of her certificate copy in her bag, by the neatness of her handwriting around the name Monir, by the private little corner of hope she had protected from Halima's contempt and her own intelligence. She had thought perhaps she could arrive, ask the right questions, and walk back onto Kadam Char with something solid: a workplace, an ID number, a witness, a man gone old enough to be sorry. Instead she had found thirteen days of tea, a kerosene bottle, fever water, a lungi, a blue bag that might not have been his, and a southbound boat that could have taken anybody.

"There was a woman before Noorjahan too," Selim said, looking not at Nura but out toward the dredger pipe. "At another landing. I don't remember her name."

"Then don't start a useless story," Babul said.

"It's not useless. It means he repeated."

Babul looked back at Nura. "That part matters. Not because it tells you who he was. Because it tells you what he wasn't. He wasn't a man whose name could hold a roof up."

There it was again, not pretty enough to be wisdom, too exact to ignore.

Shamima thanked them because Nura had gone quiet. On the way back from the yard she bought two guavas from a boy under a tarp and cut them with her thumbnail, salting the slices from a twist of newspaper she kept in her bag.

"Eat," she said, handing one to Nura.

"I'm not hungry."

"That's not what I asked."

Nura bit into the guava because speaking would have taken more effort. It was hard and almost sour. Salt stung the split place inside her lip where old tenderness still lived from the day men had shoved her in Halima's doorway. The sky had brightened to the bleached white that came before noon heat turned mean. Nearby, Momena's stall clattered with cups. Across the lane, Noorjahan had already gone back to her fish basin. Life had sealed itself over the conversation without pretending it had not happened. Nura respected that more than comfort would have deserved.

Before they headed to the ghat, Ruksana came hurrying after them with a twist of puffed rice in a page torn from a school copybook. "For the ride," she said, pressing it into Nura's hand. The baby was asleep on her shoulder now, drooling onto the printed sunflowers of her housecoat. "You'll get a headache on empty stomach. And don't go back asking old people for fairy tales. Ask what they wrote and who will swear to it."

Nura stared at her.

Ruksana shrugged, suddenly almost shy under the force of her own bluntness. "What? You think you're the first girl to learn that a man can be both fact and waste?"

She turned away before Nura could answer, calling to Juthi to come inside before the crows got the fish eyes. Shamima watched her go and said, "Well. That saved us one speech."

The return boat left later than Mannan had promised because Mannan had found an extra passenger with two sacks of duck feed and was not a man who respected anybody's urgency except his own. By the time they pushed off, the sun sat overhead like a hand holding the whole river down. The channel looked flatter now, crueler for being so bright. Diesel fumes thickened in the boat well. One duck-feed sack leaked a sweet dusty smell that made Nura think of school storerooms and damp grain.

Shamima sat with her knees up and the sewing-machine belt looped around her wrist like a black bracelet. She waited until the boat cleared the bank before speaking.

"You got what you came for?"

Nura looked at the water shredding off the bow. "I got enough."

"That's not the same thing."

"No."

Shamima let the answer sit. She knew when not to nag a wound. After a while she said, "You know your grandmother will say she was trying to save you from this."

Nura laughed once, and it came out ugly. "From what? Boat fare? Heat? Hearing that men lie in several districts at once?"

"From wanting a clean answer where there isn't one."

Nura unfolded the slip with Monir's name and smoothed it on her thigh. Sweat had dampened the edges. The pencil line was still dark. That, more than anything, enraged her. The name held better than the man ever had. Paper survived by being carried carefully, dried, recopied, hidden from rain. Men like Monir survived by stepping out of one landing and onto another until nobody could prove where they belonged. Halima had spent years teaching paper to do the work people failed to do. Maybe that was mercy. Maybe it was another way of cornering a life before the person inside it could choose.

She thought of Noorjahan's doorway, Ruksana's water tumbler, Momena's biscuit carton covered in credit marks. None of those women had waited for authority before building a way to last. They had not been freer than Halima. They had simply been distributed. The work of keeping one another legible was spread across neighbors, cups, children, borrowed plastic, a memory for debt. Nura had grown up inside Halima's house believing the old woman's ledger was a singular dangerous thing. Maybe it was dangerous because it was singular. Too much of one person's choosing pressed onto too many other lives.

The boat slammed down from a chop of current and spray hit her calves. Mannan cursed the engine, then patted the skullcap back into place as if decorum could steady the river. The extra passenger fell asleep sitting up with his cheek against the duck-feed sack. Shamima chewed guava seeds and watched Nura from the corner of her eye.

"So what now?" she asked.

Nura folded the slip once, then again. She did not tear it. That surprised her.

"Now I stop asking for a father," she said.

Shamima raised her brows. "That easy?"

"No. Not easy. Just useless."

She said it and felt the truth of it scrape through her. There would still be nights when she would want a face to put under the name, a body to blame, some man she could hate without having to divide the hatred among the dead, the missing, and the women who had chosen for her. There would still be the childish stab of seeing other people produce fathers like proof of purchase. But a useless want was still useless. She had seen the scale of Monir now: tea debts, borrowed kerosene, and a blue bag maybe. That was all the factual fatherhood on offer.

What remained was not cleaner. It was only hers to ask for.

She did not need Halima to tell her who he was. She needed Halima to tell her what she had written, who could witness it, and what kind of lie would still let Nura get on with her life.

The river widened. Kadam Char came back into view in pieces: a run of tin roofs, the school wall with its pasted notices lifting at the corners, the mosque courtyard gone pale in noon glare, children no bigger than fingers on the bank. It was home only if that word still meant the place where a person had to argue for room. Nura felt no relief at the sight of it. She felt only readiness, and under that a harder anger than the one that had sent her out in the dark. Before, she had been angry because Halima had denied her choice. Now she was angry because Halima had made the choice first and then kept the method hidden, as if gratitude should cover the theft.

Mannan nosed the boat into the mud. Shamima stood, slung her bag over one shoulder, and said, "If she starts shouting, come get me after. Not during. I don't fight old women before lunch."

For the first time that day, Nura smiled properly.

"Coward."

"Practitioner," Shamima said, stepping off.

Nura followed her into the sucking mud at the bank. The puffed rice crackled in the copybook paper under her arm. The folded slip with Monir's name sat hot inside her palm. By the time she had climbed the path toward the house, the question that had brought her across the channel was already dead. The one she carried back was harder and would not wait: not where is Monir, but what did Halima put into my name, and what will it take to make a life stand on it without asking any vanished man to claim me.


# Chapter 13

By the time Halima reached the schoolroom, Aminul had pushed the children's benches into two hard ranks and wiped the blackboard clean except for four chalk headings: NAME, RELATION, YEARS IN HOUSE, WITNESSES. The room smelled of old dust, damp jute, and the sour chalk children sucked when the teacher's back was turned. There had been no rain since yesterday, but the river still made itself known. From the eastern side of the char came, now and then, the low collapsing sound of bank giving way somewhere out of sight, as if a huge wet mat were being dragged over earth.

Nura sat at the teacher's table with ruled paper stacked by her elbow, the small burn mark on her wrist showing each time she dipped her pen. She had tied her hair back too tight. Since the trip to the neighboring char she had moved with a colder economy, as if she had spent a day looking for a father and come back with no use left for softness. She did not look up when Halima came in. She only said, "He's made groups," and slid her chin toward Aminul.

Aminul stood near the board with his folders held a little off his shirtfront to save them from sweat. Even in this heat he had managed a pressed collar. Chalk dust sat on one cuff. "The clerk sent word from the mainland," he said. "The magistrate may come the day after tomorrow. If not then, the next morning. We don't have room for confusion now."

Halima put her palm on the nearest bench. The wood still held the sticky warmth of children. "A house isn't an exam answer," she said.

"No," Aminul said. "But if five people from one yard give me five versions, the affidavit will tear in the first question."

He had arranged the first pile already, with weak papers on top and difficult dependencies underneath, the households most likely to split open when asked to sound official sitting at the very front. He did not say that part aloud, but Halima saw it in the order of the names. He had learned the island well enough to sort danger. That, more than his folders or his cautious beard, made her distrust him.

The first group came in with their hesitation still on them: Rashid with net tar dried black on his cuffs, his wife Parvin carrying their younger boy on one hip, and Khodeja fupu a half step behind them, counting the tablets knotted in the corner of her sari by touch. Khodeja had no business in a schoolroom this early; she usually spent the morning on Rashid's veranda sorting lentils with her bad eyes close to the grain, pausing every few minutes to spit clove into the yard. Today she had oiled the white hair at her temples flat and put on a blouse with one intact hook. She had come as if appearance could keep her from being discussed like a cracked pot.

"Sit," Aminul said, then corrected himself when he saw Khodeja searching for the bench with her feet. "Please. Slowly."

Khodeja lowered herself with both hands and a small animal sound she tried to hide inside her throat. Halima had heard that sound from women laboring, from old men with water in the lungs, from goats dragged by a broken leg. It was the body's unwilling truth. No paper ever held it.

Aminul glanced at the page. "We'll start plain. Give full names, then the relation, then how long you've stayed under one roof. After that I ask the questions the magistrate is likely to ask."

Rashid said his own name first, then Parvin's, then the two boys. When he got to Khodeja he paused just long enough for Halima to feel it in the bench under her hand.

"She's my father's sister," he said.

"And she lives with you?" Aminul asked.

"She stays there," Rashid said.

Parvin shifted the child higher on her hip. "Mostly," she said. "Sometimes she goes west to Khaleka's place."

Khodeja's fingers stopped moving over the medicine knot.

Halima turned her head. "When did she last go west?"

Parvin did not look at her. "I mean, she could. If needed."

Aminul kept his voice level. He was careful like this when he knew a floorboard was rotten. "Listen to me properly. An adult dependent with no clear paper line creates questions. I'm not making a moral point. I'm telling you the risk. If you include her, you need a clean answer for why she's part of the household and who can witness it. If you don't include her, then don't include her anywhere. Don't wobble."

The younger boy had found the chalk tray and was scraping at it with one dirty fingernail. The sound set Halima's teeth on edge.

Rashid said, "The share gets thinner if the household gets bigger. Everyone knows that."

No one answered. The sentence lay in the schoolroom like a bad smell.

Khodeja spoke before Parvin could. Her voice had the rubbed-down grain of an old broom. "Leave me out, then. I can sit with Khaleka till the list goes through. I'm old. I don't need much."

Parvin said too quickly, "No one said that."

"You said enough," Halima said.

Nura's pen stopped. Even Aminul looked up from the page.

Halima stepped closer to Khodeja. She could smell clove, stale cough syrup, and the faint sourness of old cloth dried too slowly. Last winter she had wrapped the old woman's shin after a fall from the veranda step; the skin there had torn like wet paper and healed shiny. Rashid's wife had changed the bandage each morning before cooking. Halima had seen the yellow medicine stains on the family's washing line for a week. Now Parvin stood three feet away pretending this woman floated in and out like weather.

"Where does she sleep?" Halima asked.

Rashid stared at the floor.

"Tell it," Halima said.

"Near the rice drum," he muttered.

"Who gives her tablets?"

Parvin swallowed. "I do."

"Who washed her when she couldn't bend after the fall?"

Parvin's face tightened. "I did."

Halima put her palm flat on the table, not hard, but enough to stop the room from sliding further into cowardice. "Then don't sit here teaching your own mouth to forget her."

Aminul rubbed the chalk dust from his thumb onto his trousers. "And if the magistrate asks why she has no supporting line through husband or sons?"

Halima did not turn toward him. "Then they say the truth. Her husband died. Her son went under in flood season and came back bloated to another char. She has eaten from this house since before that boy there stopped pissing the bed."

The younger boy looked up at the word and grinned. Khodeja did not. Her eyes had gone watery, but she was too proud to let tears fall in front of the others. She only pinched the knot of tablets until the pills made a hard little shape against the cloth.

Aminul said, "Truth isn't the only issue. Sequence is. If one witness says she arrived after the son died and another says before, the clerk will start digging for contradiction. That's what I'm trying to prevent."

"Then prevent contradiction," Halima said. "Don't prevent the woman."

For a moment Aminul looked tired enough to drop the official courtesy from his face. Then he nodded once. "Fine. Again." He lifted his pen. "Rashid. Start from the top. Full names."

This time Rashid said Khodeja's name without swallowing it. The words were not warmer for being repeated, but they stayed in the room. Halima took what she could get.

When the group stood to leave, Khodeja gripped Halima's wrist with fingers dry as fishbone. "Dai-ma," she whispered, keeping her mouth turned away so the others would not hear, "don't waste the government's tin on me."

Halima bent close enough for the old woman to smell the onion she had cut before leaving home. "You hush. You've taken enough less in this life."

Khodeja made a face that might have been agreement or phlegm and shuffled out, one hand still pressed to the medicine knot like she could count herself there if nowhere else.

The next households came faster. Once people understood the rehearsal was real, they did not treat it like advice. They treated it like weather. They braced.

By midmorning the schoolroom had grown hot enough that the chalk lines on the board began to sweat into the paint. Aminul called names, Nura copied statements in a cleaner hand than anyone else on the char could manage, and Halima listened to lives being pressed flatter and flatter so they would slide more easily into official ears.

Bilkis came in with her son Samu on her lap, smelling faintly of stale milk because she had weaned him too quickly in hopes of shrinking herself back down before the hearing. The boy banged a spoon against his own ankle and laughed each time the bone answered. Aminul asked for the marriage date. The room shifted the way rooms always did when sex had to be turned into calendar language in front of men. Bilkis picked at the loose thread on her sleeve.

"He was born early," she said.

From the doorway her mother-in-law said, "Not that early."

Aminul closed his eyes briefly. "Please don't help from the doorway unless asked."

"I'm asked every day by the village," the older woman said, but she shut her mouth after that.

Halima watched Bilkis's face. Shame had put a shine on it that was not sweat. She remembered the birth: the child slippery and furious, the husband not yet fully hers in the eyes of the papers, the mother-in-law counting months more angrily than she had counted prayers. Back then Halima had done what she always did. She had wrapped the baby, cut the cord, and dared the household to love him faster than the arithmetic.

"Say this," Halima told Bilkis, not unkindly. "The marriage paper came late because the imam was marooned by flood. You entered the household before the paper, not after the man. That's common enough that no one can prove it strange."

Aminul's mouth tightened, but he wrote it. He knew as well as she did that there were two kinds of truth on the char: the one bodies remembered and the one offices could survive. Most days the two hated each other.

Jalal's household came just before noon and left a worse taste. Jalal himself wore a lungi with engine grease at the knee and kept cracking his knuckles as if the sound might harden him. His wife Mina had painted her toenails red once for Eid and the color still clung in stubborn islands. With them came Mina's younger sister Munni and Munni's daughter Tuli, who carried a school slate wrapped in a scarf and stared at the room as if she expected to be tested on letters.

Munni had a habit of smoothing the child's hairline with a damp thumb every few breaths, even when it was already flat. The girl tolerated it with the patient contempt of children who know their mothers are frightened.

Aminul asked the questions in order: names, relations, years in the house, witnesses.

Jalal answered for everyone until Aminul stopped him. "Let each person speak for themselves," he said.

Mina said Munni had been with them since her husband disappeared downriver on dredging work. She said it in a thin voice, as if disappearance were a technical term and not the shape of half the men's futures on the chars. When Aminul asked whether Munni and the child were part of the household claim, Mina's mouth pinched.

"She helps with work," Mina said. "She stays because where else will she go? But it's not permanent in that sense."

Munni nodded too fast. "I can stay elsewhere till the hearing if that helps."

Tuli looked up at her mother. "Where?"

No one answered. The schoolroom had gone still except for a fly worrying itself against the window grille.

Aminul said, careful again, "If they're in, they're in. If they're out, they're out. If I present them as temporary labor and a visiting child, you all have to say the same thing without breaking under questions. Understand me."

Nura read back what he had written, her voice flat with disgust she kept tucked inside grammar. "Munni Begum. Temporary resident. Supports herself through domestic help. Child not economically dependent on claimant household."

The sentence made Tuli frown. "I eat there," she said.

Jalal snapped, "Quiet."

But Tuli had already stepped into the crack. She hugged the slate to her chest and looked at Nura instead of the adults. "I sleep by Khala, near the wall where lizards fall from the tin."

Even Aminul's pen paused.

Halima felt the whole room tilt toward the child and away again. That was witness preparation too: teaching a girl exactly when adults preferred her to stop existing.

Munni laughed once, a dry wrong sound. "She's just saying whatever she sees."

"She sees where she sleeps," Halima said.

Mina rounded on her, all the strain of the last weeks showing through the careful wife-face. "And if we list every hungry person who has slept on our floor this year, what then? Will the government give us another room at Nabinpara? Another cooking pot? More rice? Or do we all stand in line for one smaller mercy?"

It was the first honest sentence spoken in the room all day, and because it was honest Halima had no easy answer ready.

Aminul took it as permission to keep going. "This is what I'm saying. The magistrate won't reward confusion. He won't reward generosity either, if it can't be defended. If Munni and the child are permanent dependents, then say that and stand by it. If not, don't improvise tenderness in the hearing and expect the file to carry it."

Munni stared at the board. Nura's copy lay on the table, the words temporary resident still wet enough to shine. Halima remembered Munni three months earlier with blood up to both wrists, helping press Mina's belly after the youngest was born, steady as a trained hand though no one had ever paid her for the steadiness. She remembered Tuli asleep under the same mosquito net as Mina's sons while the rain beat the tin all night. Temporary, Aminul had written. As if storm seasons could be measured cleanly from inside an office.

"Cross that out," Halima said.

No one moved.

"Cross it out," she said again, this time to Nura.

Nura met her eyes for a second, then drew a hard line through temporary resident so deep the nib nearly tore the paper.

Jalal looked from one face to the next and found no rescue. "If we claim them," he said, "and the share breaks smaller, who's feeding everybody after?"

"You already are," Halima said.

"Not under a clerk's eye."

That was the whole thing. Men could live one life in a yard and another in a file for years, but once an office stared at the join between them, the seam began to bleed.

The call to noon prayer drifted thinly from the mosque, and for a few minutes the schoolroom emptied enough for breath. People stood outside in clumps under the hard white light, arguing in low voices they imagined the room could not hear. It heard everything. Children passed at the doorway and peered in at the blackboard as if it were a game. One of them had drawn a boat in the corner of the last page Nura had copied. She blotted it out with the side of her hand before Aminul saw.

When Majed and Jamila came after the break, they brought the opposite problem: too much control. Jamila had dressed for seriousness in a washed sari with a narrow dark border and sat so straight her spine looked nailed together. Majed smelled of engine oil even after scrubbing. He kept running his thumb over the split skin beside his nail as if counting how much work waited at home.

"Please write it exactly as the papers say," Jamila told Aminul. Her voice was calm enough to cut skin. "There won't be any confusion from our side."

Halima heard the cost in that there won't. Jamila had built a whole adult life inside a paper lineage that had once saved her. Now she spoke of it like a wall she meant to keep standing even if someone had to stay outside it. Halima could not blame her. That was the rot underneath every rehearsal. Nobody here was wicked enough for the work being asked of them, but most were frightened enough.

By afternoon Aminul's blackboard had turned into a map of subtraction. It was not whole households gone at once. It was smaller things. A widowed aunt shifted to a cousin's yard for "a few days." A sister renamed hired help. A boy whose mother had remarried told to sleep at the ghat with his fishing uncle till the hearing passed so no one would have to swear he belonged under the first roof. Every adjustment was tiny when spoken aloud. Together they made the char feel meaner than it had in the morning.

Halima had spent thirty years doing the opposite work. A girl abandoned after marriage came back with a swollen belly and no safe father's name for the child, so Halima found one that would hold. A nephew whose brother died on a boat began eating from his uncle's pot, and she taught the household to stop saying guest. A widow moved her mat closer to the kitchen and became, by repetition, part of the family. It had never been clean work. It had been stitched from fear, compromise, pity, and the practical knowledge that hunger did not wait for legal elegance. Still, it had pulled people inward. What Aminul called rehearsal was teaching the island a different hand movement entirely.

When Munni and Tuli came back in near the end of the afternoon to "confirm the revised version," Halima knew the lesson had taken.

Munni did not sit this time. She kept the child's scarf bundle in one hand and spoke with a politeness so flat it made Halima's back ache.

"Tuli will stay with my elder cousin near the tea stalls for a few nights," she said. "I'll help Mina apa in daytime only. That's cleaner."

Tuli had one blue clip left in her hair; the other hung from the scarf by a thread. She did not cry. She only pressed her cheek to Munni's hip and looked at the floor with the exhausted dignity of a much older person.

"Cleaner for who?" Halima asked.

Munni gave the small shrug women learned after too many humiliations. "For the sentence."

No one laughed. Aminul scratched something onto the margin and refused to meet Halima's eyes.

When the room finally thinned, when the last claimant had gone to fetch a witness or hide from one, Halima stepped out to the veranda because the air inside had become unbreathable. The schoolyard was white with late light. Goats nosed at the fence posts. Somewhere beyond the houses a hammer rang on tin, a sharp practical sound from someone already preparing to move.

Aminul followed after a minute, carrying the board duster as though he had forgotten it was in his hand. Up close the care in his appearance had come undone. Sweat had darkened the collar seams. Chalk had settled in the trim of his moustache.

"You think I don't see what this is doing," he said.

Halima leaned one shoulder against the wall. The rough plaster caught at her blouse. "You see it and keep teaching it."

"I'm not teaching cruelty."

"No? Then what did we do all day?"

He set the duster on the windowsill with more force than necessary. Dust rose from it and drifted back into the room. "We prepared people for the kind of questions they're going to face whether you like it or not. If I don't force consistency here, the magistrate will force it there, in public, with no patience and less mercy."

Halima looked past him into the schoolroom. Nura was gathering the copied pages into piles, aligning their corners with sharp taps against the table. Each tap sounded to Halima like a door being shut.

"Mercy," she said. "You call this mercy."

"I call it triage."

The word hit wrong in her ear. It belonged to clinics, to road accidents, to choices made over bleeding people because there were not enough hands. Aminul had meant to sound practical. Instead he sounded like a man who had spent too long among categories and begun trusting them more than flesh.

"These aren't broken limbs on a floor," she said. "They're households."

"And households can still be excluded entirely." His own temper had frayed now; the balance in his sentences was gone. "Tell me I'm wrong. Tell me the magistrate will sit there all day and listen to every cousin, every foster child, every woman who came back from a vanished husband and never left again. He won't. He wants a list he can sign before the launch schedule shifts. I'm trying to get people through the mouth of a bottle."

Halima thought of Khodeja pinching her tablets through the sari knot, of Tuli saying where lizards fell from the tin, of Bilkis having to locate the shame of her son's conception on a calendar while men watched. The schoolroom had stripped ordinary acts of care and made them beg for classification.

"And to make them fit," she said, "you want them to practice leaving each other out before anyone even asks."

"They were doing that already."

"Not like this."

Aminul dragged a hand over his face and came away with chalk on the side of his thumb. "You think your way doesn't cut? It does. Lived complexity, as you call it, still becomes testimony in the end. It still becomes a signature. It still becomes a decision that pays one household and leaves another waiting outside appeals."

She heard the fatigue in him then, the sympathy his job never allowed him to display except as irritation. That was the worst of him and the best: he was not blind. He simply kept going after seeing.

"I know it cuts," she said. Her voice had dropped without her meaning it to. "I've been cutting and stitching this island together since before you learned to shave. But don't stand there and tell me this is truth against lie. It isn't. It's one kind of wound against another. It's imperfect recognition against strategic cruelty. And cruelty is easier to coach."

He did look at her then. Not as an officer. As a man who had perhaps been waiting for someone to say the ugliest thing plainly.

"Strategic cruelty may still get a family housed," he said.

"At whose door does the old woman then die? In whose yard does the child return when the cousin gets tired of feeding her?" Halima pushed herself off the wall. "You can clear a file by moving a mat. You can't clear the hunger that follows it."

For a second she thought he might answer with the hard institutional sentence, the one about limits and regulations and what could or could not be entertained. Instead he looked out toward the western huts where the poorer houses crouched low and separate, as if distance alone might make them invisible to the school wall.

"What would you have me do?" he asked, almost quietly. "Take every contradiction in raw and watch half the claims collapse?"

Halima did not answer at once. The truth was that she had no clean method to offer. Her own life disproved cleanliness. She wanted the magistrate to hear the mess of dependency exactly as it was lived: who carried water, who slept by whose wall, who came back hungry and was not turned away. She also knew what happened when men in offices met mess. They called it fraud and kept their pens dry.

Nura came to the doorway with the copied statements stacked against her chest. "Three households asked for rewrites," she said. Her voice was brisk enough to sound almost detached, but Halima knew the signs of strain in the girl now. She overcontrolled when frightened. "Khodeja fupu's bedding has been sent to Khaleka's cousin's hut on the west side. Munni and Tuli are going to stay near the tea stall. Bashir's stepson has been told to sleep on the boat till after the hearing, because apparently a child on water doesn't count the same."

She handed the papers to Aminul and went back inside before either of them replied.

That was the hearing arriving early, not on a launch or under a magistrate's seal, but in rolled mats, moved plates, and children redirected for a few nights so the sentence would come out cleaner when spoken aloud.

Across the yard, a woman passed carrying an aluminum trunk on her head while an old man walked beside her with a cane and no pace left in him. Near the gate, a boy dragged a bedding roll twice his own width through the dust, leaving a stripe behind him like something erased. The river kept giving its distant soft collapses from the east, patient as paperwork.

Halima stood with chalk grit on her palm and understood at last that the coming hearing would not sort truth from falsehood in any way that mattered to the people living here. It would reward whichever version of care could be made to sound orderly without breaking in the witness box. She had spent half her life pulling extra bodies inside when weather, men, and money tried to shake them loose. Now the schoolroom had begun teaching the char the opposite lesson. Households rolled the mat, shifted the child, said she only came sometimes. By sunset several households had already learned it well enough to act.


# Chapter 14

By the time the last witness left the schoolroom, the floor mats smelled of old sweat, damp jute, and the chalk Aminul kept rubbing off his fingers onto his trousers. All afternoon people had stood in front of the blackboard and practiced saying shame in ways that sounded administrative. A sister who had slept for eleven years in her brother-in-law's house had been taught to call herself a temporary guest. An aunt who had raised three children through two flood seasons had become, on Aminul's page, "not a regular dependent." Halima had sat through it with her bad eye watering from dust and rage, listening to lived years narrowed until they could pass under a magistrate's pen.

Sufia came in near the end with a kettle hooked in one cloth-thickened hand and six glasses trapped between the fingers of the other. She always sorted the glasses by cracks, tapping each rim with her thumbnail before she poured, as if she could hear which one would split under hot tea and which one would last another week. The boys at the ghat called her stingy for that. They did not know what six broken glasses cost a stall that already sold more on promise than on cash.

She poured for Aminul first, because he was the man with files. Then for the two men from the eastern side who had spent an hour deciding whether an old widowed aunt ate enough in their yard to count. Then for Nura, who had been copying names at the edge of the desk with the quick, angry neatness she used when she wanted not to speak. When Sufia reached Halima she tilted the glass wrong and the tea slopped over her own knuckles.

"Your hand's gone blind before your eye has," Halima said.

Sufia sucked tea off the base of her thumb and gave the little dry smile she used when she wanted a sentence to stay neighborly. "It's been a bad day for all body parts. The ferry boys broke one spoon. Your Nura wrote through two nibs. That old Karim uncle farted in God's own face and blamed the bench."

Nura snorted despite herself. Aminul looked up from his papers, not sure whether he was allowed to hear that. Halima kept her eyes on Sufia. The woman had missed her look twice already. That was unlike her. Sufia lived by noticing what other people wished she would miss.

Halima took the glass but did not drink. "Come to the house after evening prayer."

Sufia set the kettle down too hard. The glasses chimed against one another. "House? For what?"

"To answer something."

"Everything needs answering these days." Sufia bent to gather empty saucers that did not need gathering. "I've got a stall to run, Halima-dai. Men still drink tea while their households collapse. Maybe more because of it."

"Then answer here."

That made Sufia look up. Not long. Long enough.

The schoolroom had thinned. One of the eastern men was still arguing with Aminul about whether a daughter's marriage counted as household exit if she kept coming back to eat. Nura was stacking used papers and blowing grit off the top sheet. Outside, children were dragging a broken bench across the yard. The sound went through the wall like a saw.

Sufia lowered her voice. "Not in front of chalk and boys."

Halima set the tea untouched on the desk. "Then where?"

Sufia wiped her hands down the front of her sari, leaving two darker lines in the cloth. "When I close the stall. Behind it. After the last boat."

"What am I waiting to hear?"

Sufia gave a short laugh with no air in it. "If I knew how to say it fast, I'd have said it already."

That was when Halima understood the thing had shape. Not rumor. Not some boy's theft or some party man's rummaging. Something with a face she knew.

She left Sufia to her kettle and stayed through the end of the rehearsal because leaving early would have started ten new stories. Aminul ran the final household again, trying to get a husband to say his stepdaughter had lived with him since age six instead of age eight because the school record would hold better. The man kept shaving the girl smaller with every sentence. Halima stopped him only when he got down to four.

"She wasn't born in your fishing basket," she said. "Stop cutting her to fit your mouth."

No one answered. Nura's pen stopped. Aminul made a mark in the margin and did not look at Halima.

By dusk the schoolyard had gone copper with dust and low sun. The air was drier now than it had been a week ago, but the char still held its smell of wet silt underneath everything, as if the mud had merely pulled a thin skin over itself and was waiting to be stepped through. Halima walked toward the ghat with the taste of unsipped tea and anger sitting together in her mouth.

Sufia's stall stood where it always did, just above the landing slope, half bamboo, half tin, its front bench listing because one leg had sunk farther into the earth than the others. She had folded an old election leaflet under that leg months ago and kept kicking it back into place whenever customers rocked the bench loose. She did it now without looking, heel finding paper by habit. Her limp was worse when she was tired. She hid it in company by moving faster.

Boatmen were taking their last tea before dark. A boy in a school shirt chewed betel and pretended he was old enough to spit red. Sufia worked the kettle, cut limes, counted coins, and did not once look toward Halima. She filled silence with business.

"The milk turned sour by noon," she said to no one and everyone. "Whoever brought that can must hate my father's soul. You, pay now or drink river water tomorrow. No, not tomorrow, tonight. Your debt already has grandchildren."

The men laughed. Sufia laughed with them. Her hands stayed quick. Only the skin at the base of her throat moved too fast.

Halima waited. She had delivered babies in smaller spaces than that stall, had stood between women's knees while mothers-in-law, goats, rain, and screaming children crowded the same room. Waiting did not tire her. It tired other people. At last the final boat pushed off and the last two men drained their glasses and went up the slope still arguing about rope prices. Sufia rinsed the kettle once, wiped the counter, closed the biscuit tin, and dropped the day's coins into a rusted tobacco box.

"Come then," she said without turning. "If I'm going to ruin my own evening, I might as well get on with it."

She led Halima behind the stall to the narrow strip where Sufia kept firewood under a bent sheet of corrugated tin. A torn fishing net hung there to dry. Beyond it the river took up the dark, flat and wide and never still. Water slapped the moored boats below in small patient blows. Somewhere farther down the bank a child was crying in the thin, affronted way of a child who had not yet understood that nobody was coming quickly.

Sufia lowered herself onto an upturned crate and rubbed once at the bad ankle through her sari. Halima stayed standing. The tin behind them still held the day's heat.

"Say it," Halima said.

Sufia looked at the river first, not at her. "You remember that flood year when the water took the goat sheds from the west side and everybody had to lift trunks onto charpoys? The year you went house to house telling women to sleep with the papers under their blouses if they had to?"

"I remember every year. Pick one."

"The year after Saleha died."

The name hit Halima low and hard, the way an elbow caught by accident in a crowd could hurt more than a planned blow. Sufia saw it land and did not look away this time.

"I copied them," she said.

The river kept slapping the boat hull. A rope strained and eased. Halima heard each sound separately, too clearly.

"What did you copy?"

"You know what."

"Make your mouth do the work."

Sufia swallowed. When she was frightened she usually packed the air with ordinary detail until the fear lost its edges. Halima could hear her trying now.

"The kettle cracked that season. Remember? I was boiling tea in that black pot with no handle. My boy had fever. Mofiz was bringing men from the union office around because of that survey on the eastern side. Everybody was saying land lines would be measured proper this time, not guessed from whoever shouted loudest. You had that fever after the twins. You were talking in your sleep. I sat there and thought if you died, half this char would wake up without names."

She stopped. Halima let the silence sit. Sufia had used the wrong road on purpose, walking around the thing until shame tired her out. Let it.

"I copied selected pages," Sufia said at last. "Not the whole ledger. I couldn't. My hand cramped and my spelling was already ugly enough to insult the dead. I took the pages that touched the eastern high ground and the households always arguing over who belonged under which roof. I made copies at night when I helped you sort things. Sometimes when you were bathing. Once while you slept."

The last sentence came out small. Not because it was small. Because it was the one that showed where the knife had entered.

Halima felt the old labor calluses in her palms as if she were gripping someone's knees. She could see Sufia at nineteen, a thin-armed girl with lamp smoke in her hair, holding hot water while Halima cut cords. She could see Sufia later, broadening, slowing in one leg, still good with frightened mothers and still quicker than most women with a clean cloth. She had let this woman into blood rooms, into the hour when a woman's insides were still partly outside her, into the little stupid jokes women made while waiting for pain. She had not let her into the ledger.

"Why those pages?" Halima asked.

"Because those were the ones Mofiz said would matter if the survey men came back. He said the higher ground would decide who got pushed west and who kept their bamboo. He said if there wasn't a second trace anywhere, one bad leak in your roof and whole households would disappear into mud."

"So you handed him a second trace."

"I handed him what I thought was a backup."

Halima laughed once, harsh enough to startle even herself. "To Mofiz."

"Yes. To Mofiz." Sufia's chin lifted. Not in pride. In refusal to beg yet. "He had a steel trunk then. He had dealings with offices. Men listened when he said paper should be kept dry. You had one oilcloth bundle under a roof every gossiping fool on the char could point to."

"I kept it out of men's hands for a reason."

"I know why you kept it." Sufia's voice sharpened for the first time. "Don't talk to me like I found it under a bush. I was there when you wrote half those lines. I held the lamp. I know which father was made from mercy and which from fear and which from the need to keep a girl out of a house that would've eaten her. I know."

She breathed through her nose, once, twice. When she spoke again the sideways humor was gone.

"I also knew one woman was holding too much."

That hit differently. Halima had been ready for accusation, for apology, even for some miserable little confession that Sufia had taken money. This was worse because it had bone in it.

"You think I wanted it?" Halima said. "You think I sat up nights rubbing my hands because the whole char had to come through my door?"

Sufia shook her head. "Wanting isn't the only way to keep power."

The tin at Halima's back gave off stored heat. Sweat slid once down her spine and stopped under the waistband of her petticoat. She did not move.

Sufia went on, and now that she had stepped onto the straight road she stayed there.

"When women came to you, they came because they had to. A husband gone to debt work, a child born too soon after marriage, a girl who needed a father line that wouldn't get her sold, a widow who needed a brother's name to keep rice coming. You helped them. I'm not spitting on that. But it was still your door. Your decision. Your memory. If the book burned, all of it burned with you. That scared me. It scared me more because you acted like fear of that meant a person was weak."

Halima opened her mouth, then shut it. What came first was not a rebuttal but a row of bodies. Women crouched on her floor with wet hems. Men who could not meet her eye until they needed her to make a child belong somewhere. Grandmothers pressing coins into her hand to ask whether a date might move by two months if it saved a daughter. She had called it work because it was work. Blood work. Paper work. Women's after-midnight work done while officials slept. But every one of those people had still sat and waited while she decided what could be altered and what could not bear touch.

She had never taken bribes the way Mofiz did. She had never turned a widow out to enlarge a land strip. She had never made a line for profit. All of that remained true. Truth could sit beside another truth without canceling it. That was part of the misery.

"What else?" she said.

Sufia picked a splinter from the edge of the crate and rolled it between her fingers. "He used them."

Halima had known that was coming. Knowing did not soften it.

"Tell it properly."

"Whenever there was a quarrel over the eastern side, he had those copies in plastic. Not the whole page. Just enough. He'd peel off a line and feed it to whoever needed feeding. This child was born under that roof, so the mother had entered that household before the old man died. This daughter had already married out, so she couldn't split a homestead later. This younger boy had no separate father line worth counting, so he stayed folded into the bigger house. Little lines. You know how much a little line can hold."

Halima knew.

"He started thickening his own people with them," Sufia said. "Not always by name. By arrangement. By who had been attached to him long enough that he could sound inevitable. A widow here. A nephew there. A tenant turned kin when kin was useful. He made old improvisations sit under his hand like they were law. And because some of those lines came from your mercy, they looked old. Respectable. Already lived in."

The river smell thickened as the evening cooled, damp metal and fish oil and silt. Halima could almost see the copied pages, not as paper but as the births behind them. A baby rubbed blue to pink while rain hammered tin. A girl child registered under a safer uncle because the real father drank through every wage and had already promised one daughter to a creditor's son. A boy slid into a brother's household on paper because otherwise the dead man's debts would have reached him before his teeth were all in. Those were the kinds of bends she had made. Necessary bends, many of them. Not clean. Necessary.

And Mofiz had taken the bends and turned them into walls.

"He couldn't have done that without wanting it already," Halima said.

"No." Sufia looked at her then, direct enough to hurt. "But he couldn't have done it without me either."

That, finally, sounded like confession rather than argument.

Halima thought of the search at her house. The stomp of sandals, the young men's hands in bedding and rice tins, Nura's split lip. She had spent days thinking of theft as a sudden act, something taken in one afternoon by men who smelled blood. Sufia was showing her the slower theft underneath it, the one done years ago in lamplight by a woman with tired hands who thought she was preventing a future disaster and was, in fact, storing one.

"Did he pay you?" Halima asked.

Sufia flinched as if slapped, then recovered enough to answer straight. "Not like that."

"That means he paid you some way."

"He kept my stall where it was when the ghat got shifted. He talked to the ferry boys when they got drunk and started taking tea on credit they'd never settle. He made me feel like I wasn't just passing cups, like I had a place in matters bigger than milk and lime." Her mouth twisted. "There. That's uglier than money. Happy?"

Halima was not happy. The answer, though, made sense in the sour ordinary way human failure usually made sense. Fear had one pocket. Vanity had another. Need lived in both.

"Why tell me now?"

Sufia spat neatly into the mud away from the firewood, red betel darkening to brown almost at once. When she answered, her voice had gone flat.

"Because he's getting ready to use them at the hearing if the room turns against him. He showed me yesterday. Not to confess. To frighten me. He had three copied sheets in a plastic sleeve with the corners taped. He said if you started preaching household truth in front of the magistrate, he'd remind the room what kind of truth you wrote with. He'll say your book shifts to suit whoever is in front of you. He'll stain the ledger enough to keep his own pieces useful."

Halima did not speak.

"He thinks because I gave them, I'll stand where he tells me if he needs witness. I won't." Sufia swallowed again. "I came to you instead. Late. Cowardly late. But not that far."

For a moment Halima wanted the easy thing: to call her rotten, call her sold, call her the kind of woman who could stand beside births for years and still miss what mercy cost. The words rose. None of them stayed true long enough to say.

"What did you think a backup was?" Halima asked quietly. "Paper doesn't stay innocent because you make a second copy."

Sufia put both hands flat on her knees. The tea-stall hands. Quick, scarred, strong at the thumb web from lifting kettles and pounding betel. Once those same hands had pressed a slippery newborn against a mother's breast while Halima stitched a tear. Once they had turned a breech baby a little lower when Halima told her to brace. There was nothing villainous about them. That was the worst of it.

"I thought a second copy meant the truth wouldn't drown with one old woman," Sufia said. "I didn't think hard enough about which truth, or whose hands. I thought if I tucked some of it near power, power might protect it. I was stupid. And I was frightened. Both are true."

Halima leaned one shoulder against the tin at last. It burned through the damp cloth of her blouse. Beyond Sufia the black water opened and shut around the anchored boats. Somewhere uphill somebody was pounding chili on stone. Each strike carried clean in the thinning air.

"I used to think hiding it was the clean part," Halima said, mostly to herself.

"Was it?" Sufia asked.

Halima did not answer. She was seeing too many doors. Her own. The way people had crossed that yard with their worst trouble wrapped in old newspaper or silence. The way she had made them come one by one, not because she wanted an audience for their humiliation but because one by one was how mercy worked when paper had to be bent without breaking the whole village. Still: one by one meant through her. Under her eye. On her terms. She had called that protection. Perhaps it had been protection. It had also been monopoly. Not the chairman's loud kind, not the magistrate's stamped kind. A quieter kind, domestic enough to pass for duty.

She straightened from the tin.

"Who else knows you copied them?"

"No one I told. Maybe people guessed. Mofiz knows. One or two men he showed pieces to over the years, maybe. Not the whole thing."

"Does Nura know?"

Sufia shook her head hard. "No."

"Good."

The word came out harder than Halima intended. Nura's false father line was not some abstract household inconsistency. It was Saleha's death stretched forward through paper and into the girl's own mouth. Once the ledger went public, Nura's name would not stay clean at the edges. Halima knew that. She knew Jamila's would not. Kashem's would not. Rahima's would not. None of the careful silences would.

Sufia watched her thinking and seemed to understand enough not to interrupt.

At last Halima said, "If I keep it hidden now, he chooses which pieces of us get dragged out."

"Yes."

"If I bring it out myself, he still drags."

"Yes."

"Then there's no protected version left."

Sufia's face changed at that, not into relief but into the expression of a person hearing a sentence she had feared and expected both. "No," she said. "There isn't."

Halima pushed away from the tin. Her knees cracked. "You won't stand for him."

"I won't."

"You'll say what you told me if asked."

Sufia's chin went down, then up. "I will."

"And if you try to turn sideways again-"

"I know what your hand still weighs," Sufia said. There was almost humor in it, and the humor hurt too. "Go. Before I start crying like an idiot and make you hate me less."

"I don't hate you less."

"I didn't ask for less. I said before."

That, at least, sounded like Sufia. Halima left her there among the firewood and torn net and the stale tea smell sunk into bamboo.

The path home took her past the school first. The blackboard was still visible through the open door, a ghost rectangle in the darkening room. On it Aminul had written three witness headings in careful Bangla and rubbed out two of them so often the chalk dust had ground into the paint. Household head. Duration of residence. Supporting relation. All day they had been trying to shove lives under those three headings until people started trimming one another to fit. An aunt removed. A stepchild blurred. A married daughter pushed farther away from the cooking fire that had fed her half her life.

The rehearsals had felt cruel already. Sufia's confession made them look worse. Halima had thought she was deciding between hidden truth and public harm. That was gone now. Hidden truth had already leaked into the hands of a man who used it to make his own arrangements look inevitable. Privacy had not prevented contamination. It had only hidden where the contamination collected.

By the time she reached the house, the cooking fire was lit. Nura was squatting by the stove with her dupatta tucked tight at the waist, blowing under the pot ring. The rice water hissed over and ran into the ash. She looked up once, quickly.

"You're late."

Halima set her sandals by the wall. "I had talk."

"With who?"

"With Sufia."

That made Nura stop. Not because Sufia mattered in herself, maybe, but because nobody sought Sufia privately unless news had gone poisonous.

"What now?" Nura asked.

Halima did not answer at once. The house smelled of rice starch, smoke, old paper, and the medicinal oil Nura had been rubbing into her wrist where the old burn tightened in damp weather. Above them, on the loft shelf, the oilcloth bundle sat where it had sat through storms, searches, and nights when Halima had sworn she would rather let it rot than let it rule the house again.

She climbed onto the low stool, reached up, and brought it down.

"What happened?" Nura said.

"Sufia made the copies."

"The missing pages?"

"Years ago."

Nura's face sharpened at once, every line in it going thin. "For Mofiz?"

"At first, she says, for backup. To him, yes."

Nura laughed once, bitter as neem. "A backup in his pocket. That's clever."

"She thought one hidden book was too much in one place."

The sentence had come out before Halima had decided whether to give Sufia that much fairness. Nura heard it anyway.

"Was she wrong?" Nura asked.

Halima laid the bundle on the mat and unwrapped the outer cloth. The ledger inside smelled faintly of mildew, lamp soot, and the old mustard oil from Halima's hands. Alongside it were the loose copied sheets still remaining, their edges softened from years of handling. Gaps showed where others had once sat.

"She was wrong about him," Halima said.

"That wasn't what I asked."

Nura's voice had changed in the last two weeks. Not deeper. More exact. She had stopped asking for comfort in ways that could be mistaken for obedience. Halima touched the ledger cover with two fingers. The board underneath was warped from damp.

"No," she said after a while. "She wasn't wrong about the weight of it."

Nura let out the breath she'd been holding. Not triumph. Not forgiveness. Just recognition.

"What did he do with the copies?" she asked.

"Used lines from them to stiffen his own claims. Eastern side households. Land talk. Dependents folded where he wanted them. He plans to use them again if the hearing turns."

Nura sank back onto her heels. The fire snapped behind her. "Then he'll do it anyway. Whether you speak or not."

"Yes."

"And if you speak, he'll say you forged it all."

"Yes."

Nura looked at the open ledger, then at Halima. In the firelight the burn mark on her wrist shone pale and tight. "If you take that to the hearing, you're taking me too."

There was no theatricality in it. That was what made it hard. Just statement. A girl's father line, already rotten, about to be handled in public by men with notebooks.

Halima sat down opposite her. The mat pressed the knobs of bamboo through her knees.

"I know."

Nura's mouth moved once as if she might say something cruel and useful. She did not. "Did you decide already," she asked, "or are you still pretending you haven't?"

Halima almost told her not to use that tone. Waste of breath. The girl was right.

"I decided walking home."

Nura nodded once. "Then call Aminul before he finishes cleaning everybody into lies."

Halima looked up sharply.

Nura shrugged, not insolent, just tired. "What? You think I didn't hear today? If one more man says his aunt only eats with him during festivals, I'll feed him his own pen."

A laugh tried to rise in Halima and failed halfway. It left ache behind.

She rewrapped the ledger, not tightly, not for hiding. She left the knot loose and set the bundle by the door instead of on the shelf. Nura watched that more closely than any spoken sentence. On this char, where walls were thin and hands quick and mud got into everything, placement was declaration. The book by the door was not a secret anymore. It was cargo.

Aminul was still at the school when Halima arrived. He had moved one bench under the hanging bulb and spread the witness sheets there in orderly stacks. Even at that hour he held his folder away from his body as if sweat were a form of contamination he could out-discipline. The room was quieter without the day's fear in it, but not kind. Chalk dust had settled into the grain of the desks. A gecko clicked from behind the calendar pinned to the wall. Outside, the river kept speaking to itself in the dark.

Aminul looked up when she came in. His eyes went immediately to the bundle in her hands.

"You brought it out," he said.

"Sit properly," Halima said. "I didn't come for surprise on your face."

He sat. Not because she outranked him in any official way. Because by now he knew when not to waste motion. "What happened?"

"Sufia copied selected pages years ago and gave them to Mofiz. He used them to strengthen his eastern-side claims. He means to use them again at the hearing if he starts losing ground."

Aminul's expression changed in stages: disbelief first, then calculation, then the tight carefulness he put on when a fact threatened his existing plan. "Are you certain?"

"Yes."

"Did she explain how much he has?"

"Enough."

"Can she testify to it?"

"Yes, if she doesn't crawl back into fear before morning."

Aminul rubbed his moustache once. "This complicates-"

Halima set the bundle down on top of his neat stacks hard enough that the pages shifted. "Don't say complicates to me tonight."

He did not finish the sentence.

For a moment none of them spoke: not Aminul, not Halima, not the gecko behind the calendar. The hanging bulb buzzed. Somewhere outside a bicycle bell rang twice and faded.

At last Aminul said, more carefully, "If Mofiz has excerpts, then he can challenge authenticity selectively. If we rely on witness summaries alone, he can use the copies to create doubt around any household you support."

"Yes."

"If you produce the full ledger, he'll attack the whole document."

"Yes."

Aminul's gaze stayed on the oilcloth. "And if the whole document is attacked, every altered line in it becomes potential ammunition. Including your own household."

He was not warning her about honor. He was warning her about consequence, which was his better quality. Halima respected that enough not to pretend otherwise.

"I know whose lines are in my house," she said.

He let that sit. "Then what are you asking me to do?"

Halima felt suddenly, vividly, the weight of the book when it was wet. The drag of paper that had taken on river air over years. The way it had grown heavier not because ink weighed much but because lives did. She had spent three decades deciding when that weight should remain under cloth and when it should be brought to light for one person, two people, never the room. That control was gone. Sufia had broken it years ago. Mofiz had been using the broken pieces while Halima still told herself the center held.

"No more rehearsals that trim people smaller," she said. "No more teaching men which old woman to forget and which child to half-claim. Tomorrow the magistrate gets the ledger with the households, the lies, and the reasons buried in them. If Mofiz wants dirt, he can stand in the full field, not pocket handfuls."

Aminul looked at her for a long moment. In the bulb light his shirt collar had finally wilted. He looked less like an official then and more like a tired man who had spent weeks trying to make an institution behave like it had ears.

"That's not a small decision," he said.

"Nothing small is left."

He almost corrected the wording, then thought better of it. "If you read openly, you lose control of what happens next."

Halima looked at the blackboard, at the three headings still ghosting there from the day's work. Household head. Duration of residence. Supporting relation. So clean. So hungry.
